    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="68c3ca85686b46a7ba76e1f4c394410a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '61e391cc-1f82-4d01-aa05-71c027bf6aea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d99263539" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal sulfides are emerging as promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high theoretical capacity and low cost, their practical application yet face critical issues of sluggish kinetics and poor cycling stability. In this study, a reliable approach is introduced to overcome these challenges by fabrication of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC hollow nanospheres thanks to the enriched robust Fe─S─C, C─S, and C─Se bonding, which greatly benefit for enhancing both reaction kinetics and structural stability. Kinetic study combining with in situ characterization reveals that the incorporation of rich-Se into FeS<sub>x</sub> induces the formation of cationic Fe and Se vacancies, leading to abundant sites and optimized path for sodium storage. Density functional theory calculations also demonstrate how Se-rich engineering weakens carbonaceous polar C─S─Fe bonds and accelerates reaction dynamics. The as-prepared Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC can deliver a high reversible capacity of 515&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over 1250 cycles and achieve superior rate capability with maintaining capacity of 418&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work pioneers the concept of vacancy-rich functionalized nanostructures, offering a new pathway for designing advanced electrode materials for energy storage devices.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414246-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414246-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have garnered significant attention as a potential candidate for large-scale stationary energy storage due to the low cost and high abundance of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0001_R_d99263522e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science Chem Soc Rev Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large ionic radius of sodium ions (1.02&nbsp;Å) results in sluggish ion diffusion kinetics and structural instability during sodiation and desodiation, significantly hindering their practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0002_R_d99263522e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat Commun ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, designing nanostructured anode materials that can offer low volume variation, high reversible capacity, fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, and a long lifespan is crucial.</p>
         
         <p>Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) are highly competitive candidates as anode for SIBs owing to their high theoretical capacities, low cost, and nontoxicity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0003_R_d99263522e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano Mater. Today Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Phys. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, the crystal anisotropy off-stoichiometric Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S has garnered significant attention due to its favorably distorted lattice structure, which includes an ordered superstructure of Fe vacancies that provide ideal sites for ion adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0004_R_d99263522e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Natl. Sci. Rev. J. Mater. Res. Phase Transition">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0005_R_d99263522e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Acta Crystallogr., Sect. B: Struct. Sci., Cryst. Eng. Mater. J. Sulfur Chem.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to stoichiometric counterparts such as troilite (FeS) and pyrite (FeS<sub>2</sub>), pyrrhotite Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S possesses much higher electronic conductivity because it exhibits no bandgap near the Fermi level.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0006_R_d99263522e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Microsyst. Nanoeng.">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0008_R_d99263522e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the intercalation and transition reaction processes during cycling based on Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S often involve heterogeneous conversion, resulting in continuous dissolution of S-species, slow diffusion kinetics, and rapid capacity decay, particularly when used as a fast-charging anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome these obstacles, several approaches have been developed, including heterostructure design, carbon matrix modification, and heteroatom doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0008_R_d99263522e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Eng. J.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Chen et&nbsp;al. assembled Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S on a 2D layered MoS<sub>2</sub> support via heterostructure engineering. The change in electronic state from 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> to 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> significantly improves the electronic conductivity of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, resulting in rapid and high-concentration Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption phases (NayFe<sub>1-x</sub>Sy, y&nbsp;=&nbsp;2,3), which leads to a stabilized conversion product. Although the stabilized conversion intermediate product offers high specific capacity, it also reduces the lifespan due to ion aggregation from excessive insertion into a very small space. Therefore, developing a novel and effective strategy to realize stabilized intercalation and conversion reaction processes in the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S anode poses a significant challenge.</p>
         
         <p>It has been reported that incorporating foreign anion ligands to construct single-phase ternary composites has emerged as a promising strategy to adjust the electronic structure and potentially enhance the electrochemical activity of metal sulfides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0011_R_d99263522e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Selenium (Se), with a larger atomic radius compared to sulfur (S), can effectively enhance the shielding effect, allowing the valence electrons of Se species to experience less restraint from the nuclear charge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0012_R_d99263522e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, M─Se bonds exhibit weaker bond strengths than M─S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0013_R_d99263522e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, substituting sulfur with selenium could enhance the redox kinetics during conversion reactions. Building on this analysis, vacancy engineering represents a straightforward strategy to achieve long-lasting durability for Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S-based anodes with rapid charging capabilities. However, the fundamental relationship between the incorporation of selenium as an anion and the underlying reaction mechanisms remains to be fully understood.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose an in situ synchronous sulfuration/selenization/carbonization strategy to synthesize Se<sub>1-y</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>y</sub> hollow nanospheres encapsulated in selenium and sulfur co-doped carbon composites (Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC). The design of a carbon-wrapped hollow nanostructure with robust C─S and C─Se bonds not only prevents structure collapse but also creates close contact of Fe with S and Se through chemical complexation, thereby guaranteeing long-term stability during cycling. The abundant of Se vacancies and weakened strength of Fe─S bond due to the introduction of foreign Se atoms accelerate the charge transfer efficiency, improving the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC. Benefiting from these merits, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC delivers an excellent specific capacity, remarkable rate performance, and outstanding long cycling properties for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414246-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414246-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis strategy of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC hollow nanospheres is schematically illustrated in (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0001">1a</a>). First, Fe-MOF was prepared by coordinating Fe<sup>3+</sup> with trimesic acid to form spherical nanocrystals, which were regulated by PVP functional groups surfactant via a hydrothermal reaction. Subsequently, these spherical nanocrystals were coated with a carbon layer using polymeric resorcinol-formaldehyde resins (Fe-MOF@RF) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), Finally, the Se<sub>x</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>x</sub>@SC composite was formed through synchronous sulfuration/selenization/carbonization of Fe-MOF@RF using a mixture of sulfur and selenium.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414246-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8462c48c-f7f3-4485-bee8-5c29492c4b67/adfm202414246-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8462c48c-f7f3-4485-bee8-5c29492c4b67/adfm202414246-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6f7d4653-75b4-4b4c-9ba9-1267f3f0e533/adfm202414246-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8462c48c-f7f3-4485-bee8-5c29492c4b67/adfm202414246-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414246-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process, b,c) FESEM images, d,e) TEM images, f) HRTEM image of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite, g) HRTEM image of the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC composite, h–l) EDX elemental mappings Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphologies of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC recorded by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, showing a smooth and uniformly distributed carbon layer on the surface of the nanospheres. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0001">1d–e</a> reveal that Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC inherits the hierarchical framework of Fe-MOF and features a distinct hollow structure with a carbon shell layer, which is beneficial for electrolyte infiltration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0014_R_d99263522e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Chem. Eng. J.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high resolution (HRTEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0001">1f</a> reveals that the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC nanospheres possess a disordered crystal structure with an interplanar spacing of 0.269&nbsp;nm, which is consistent with the (201) crystal plane of the anisotropic off-stoichiometric phase Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, indicating that the substitution of smaller S ions (1.84&nbsp;Å) with larger Se<sup>2−</sup> ions (ionic radius 1.98&nbsp;Å) induces crystal structure distortion. The energy-dispersive X-ray spectrum (EDX) elemental mapping reveals that Fe, S, and Se are homogeneously distributed within the carbon shell layer matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0001">1h–I</a>). The EDX spectrum analysis indicates that the atomic ratio of Fe:S:Se in the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite is 2:1:1.5, which is consistent with the stoichiometric ratio of Se<sub>0.75</sub>Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>, confirming the successful incorporation of Se species into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S. It is worth mentioning that by adjusting the ratio of sulfur and selenium powders, the Se content in the Fe-based chalcogenide (Se<sub>1-y</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>@C) can be tailored, which is beneficial for improving the conductivity of the metal dichalcogenide. The morphology of the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, Se<sub>1-y</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>@SC, and FeSe@C composites were visualized by SEM and TEM, all of which show nanospheres encapsulated in a carbon layer (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S5</a>, Supporting Information). The EDX analysis results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) revealed that the ratio of S:Se are 0:1, 1:1, 1.5:1, and 1:0 for Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, Se<sub>0.5</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.5</sub>@SC, Se<sub>0.25</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.75</sub>@SC, and FeSe@C, respectively, corresponding to the ratios of the added raw materials.</p>
         
         <p>The crystalline phases of the Fe-based chalcogenide were examined using XRD. The prepared Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@C shows typical diffraction peaks at 30.3°, 33.7°, 43.5°, and 53.0°, corresponding to the (200), (2011), (2022), and (220) planes of the anisotropy off-stoichiometric phase Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (JPDCS NO 29–0726) without any impurity. It is noteworthy that the introduction of Se atoms by substituting S in Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S shifted these peaks slightly to lower angles. In the Raman spectra of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2b</a>), the peaks at 217, 280, and 390&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the E<sub>g</sub>, T<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>g</sub> modes of the combined FeS and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> crystal phase, respectively, confirming the preservation of the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S crystal phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0005_R_d99263522e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Acta Crystallogr., Sect. B: Struct. Sci., Cryst. Eng. Mater. J. Sulfur Chem.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0015_R_d99263522e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Ionics J. Mater. Chem. A Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, two characteristic peaks emerged at 1330 and 1591&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, assigned to the sp<sup>3</sup>-type disorder carbon (D) and sp<sup>2</sup>-type graphitized carbon (G) bands for the composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite, the A<sub>g</sub> mode shifted slightly from 390 to 395&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> toward the FeSe angle, and the intensity of the G band decreased. Additionally, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC are calculated to be ≈1.30 and 1.02, respectively. The higher intensity ratio value of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC demonstrates that the abundant Se-doped carbon species result in higher defects in the carbon layers of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite. These observations indicate the successful incorporation of Se atoms into the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0016_R_d99263522e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Omega">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To clarify the existence of rich structural defect induced by Se electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) was conducted. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2c</a>, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC presents an EPR signal at g&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.003, which originates from the charge state of Se vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0002_R_d99263522e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat Commun ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0013_R_d99263522e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0015_R_d99263522e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Ionics J. Mater. Chem. A Small">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0017_R_d99263522e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbon content of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC were investigated by TGA analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The weight loss percentages of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC are 55.2% and 71%, indicating carbon contents of ≈21.9% and 15.9%, respectively. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption tests were conducted to investigate the porosity of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), both Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC composites exhibit a mesoporous structure with BET surface area of 57.2 and 49.2&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The increased surface area of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC is primarily due to the introduction of Se during the heat treatment process, which releases a significant number of gases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0018_R_d99263522e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414246-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/493dcda8-9cf6-4bec-bfb1-6b2cf4dbc11c/adfm202414246-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/493dcda8-9cf6-4bec-bfb1-6b2cf4dbc11c/adfm202414246-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a18db1de-70d4-43ce-b273-456d14bb6a19/adfm202414246-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/493dcda8-9cf6-4bec-bfb1-6b2cf4dbc11c/adfm202414246-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414246-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns Se<sub>1-x</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>x</sub>@SC, b) Raman spectra, and c) electron paramagnetic resonance for Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC. High-resolution XPS spectra of d) Fe 3d and e) S 2p for Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC. f) High-resolution XPS spectrum of Se 3d in the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted to investigate the surface composition and valence states of the samples. The survey spectrum reveals the chemical composition of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a>, Supporting Information). In the high-resolution Fe 2p spectrum of the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2d</a>), seven peaks are observed. The peaks at binding energies of 711.09 and 724.28&nbsp;eV correspond to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, respectively. The peaks at 713.96, 727.12, 718.93, and 732.57&nbsp;eV are attributed to the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and two satellite peaks of Fe<sup>3+</sup>, respectively. Additionally, a small peak at 707.07&nbsp;eV corresponds to cationic Fe vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the introduction of Se atoms, no Fe vacancies were detected in the Fe 3d spectrum of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@C relative to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, suggesting a rearrangement of Fe vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0019_R_d99263522e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results confirm that the incorporation of Se atoms can modulate the electronic structure of Fe vacancies. In the S 2p high-resolution spectrum of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2e</a>), the two prominent peaks at 162.0 and 163.9&nbsp;eV belong to S 2p1/2 and Fe─S─C of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, respectively. The peak at 165.3&nbsp;eV is assigned to the C─S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0016_R_d99263522e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Omega">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0020_R_d99263522e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of Fe─S─C and C─S bonds demonstrates a strong chemical bonding between Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S and carbon shell. It is interesting the peaks of S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and the C─S bond slightly shift to lower binding energies in the S 2p spectrum of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC after the incorporation of Se atoms, confirming successful Se doping into the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S crystal and modulated the electron clouds within the S species. Additionally, the peak corresponding to the C─Fe─S bond remains in Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and slightly shift to the higher binding energies. This phenomenon is also observed in the A<sub>g</sub> modes of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC after Se incorporation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2b</a>). Besides, two peaks located at 159.7 and 165.9&nbsp;eV belong to Se 3p/2 and Se 3p1/2, respectively, indicating the valance states of S<sup>−2</sup> in the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0020_R_d99263522e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other peak at 168.1&nbsp;eV corresponds to SO<sub>x</sub>, attributed to the oxidation of S<sup>−2</sup> in the air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0021_R_d99263522e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Se 3d high-resolution spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0002">2f</a>), four peaks are observed. The peaks at 54.0 and 55.5&nbsp;eV correspond to Se 3d5/2 and Se 3d3/2respectively, while the peak at 56.6&nbsp;eV is attributed to the Se─C bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0022_R_d99263522e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the peak at 58.6&nbsp;eV is assigned to SeO<sub>x</sub>, associated with the oxidation of Se<sup>−2</sup> in the air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0022_R_d99263522e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The binding energies of C 1s at 284.7, 286.0, and 288.5&nbsp;eV align well with C─C, C─S and O═C─O bonds, implying successful sulfur doping in the carbon layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c,d</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0023_R_d99263522e1166" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results reveal that the C─Se, C─S, and C─Fe─S bonds provide robust coupling between the carbon matrix and Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>, which enhances structural integrity and accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, ensuring high capacity and long-term cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage electrochemical properties of the prepared composite anodes are evaluated in half-cells using metallic sodium foil as the counter electrode. The cyclic volumetric (CV) curves of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC are compared in the first three cycles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage window 0.4–3.0&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). During the first cathodic scan of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, a relatively weak peak emerged at 1.04&nbsp;V due to the intercalation reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, forming Na<sub>y</sub>Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and a stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. The peak at 0.85&nbsp;V is related to the formation of Na<sub>y</sub>Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, followed by the conversion reaction of NaFe<sub>1-x</sub>S to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0017_R_d99263522e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two smaller peaks located at 1.63 and 1.93&nbsp;V are corresponded to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0024_R_d99263522e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent anodic scan, two oxidation peaks at 1.28 and 1.51&nbsp;V are attributed to the reversible phase transition of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S and the deintercalation process, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The two obvious peaks located at 2.04 and 2.35&nbsp;V indicate the reversible conversion of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se, forming the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0014_R_d99263522e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Chem. Eng. J.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0024_R_d99263522e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the second and third scans of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, the sodiation peaks for the intercalation and conversion processes shift to higher and lower voltages with excellent reproducibility, respectively. In contrast, the CV of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@C exhibits a distinct curve compared to Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, indicating differing electrochemical properties (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3a</a>). Subsequently, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode maintain steady and well-overlapped redox peaks with larger CV curve areas compared to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, indicating that the incorporation of Se atoms into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC enhances the intercalation-conversion reaction and reversible sodiation/desodiation processes. The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles demonstrate that the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode delivers initial charge/discharge capacities of 535/609&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 87.9%, surpassing that of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S which achieves 455/618&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 73.6% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a,b</a>, Supporting Information). This performance exceeds that reported for other metal dichalcogenides with multiple anions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0013_R_d99263522e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0020_R_d99263522e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0025_R_d99263522e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J. Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414246-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/512324e6-01ce-4235-9523-cd2cb1ae055b/adfm202414246-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/512324e6-01ce-4235-9523-cd2cb1ae055b/adfm202414246-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f675d496-eb10-42ac-8977-bde7efddee7d/adfm202414246-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/512324e6-01ce-4235-9523-cd2cb1ae055b/adfm202414246-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414246-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC electrodes at a scan rate 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup> for the 2nd and 3rd cycles. b) Comparison of ICE and c) cycling performance for 300 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate abilities and e) reversible capacity at different rates/cycling performance of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC with the anode previously reported. f) Charge/discharge curves of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC at different current densities. g) Long-term cycling test of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, and FeSe@C electrode at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. i) Normalized charge–discharge profiles, j) charge–discharge at different current densities and k) cycling stability at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (inlet of digital photograph of different colors of LED lights powered by the NVP/C/Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The cycle performance of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, and FeSe@C at a current density 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3c</a>. The capacity of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode gradually increases after the initial cycles, which can be attributed to the continuous activation of active materials and the decomposition of electrolytes during cycling. After 300 cycles, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode delivers the highest reversible and stable capacity of 532.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> surpassing the 401.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@C. Notably, the FeSe@C exhibits a lower but more stable capacity of 297&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> compared to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, indicating that the incorporation of Se atoms can mitigate the dissolution of sulfur species. Furthermore, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode delivered reversible capacities of 507.4, 496.2, 476.7, 452.9, and 432.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 8&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Surprisingly, it achieved a reversible capacity of 418&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Upon returning to a current density of 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it retained 100% of its initial capacity, demonstrating fast redox reaction and low polarization. The rate performance is much higher than that of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@C and FeSe@C, standing out among other reported metal sulfide and metal selenide anodes for SIBs<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0013_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0022_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0025_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J. Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0026_R_d99263522e1447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). Additionally, a stable charge–discharge voltage profile and low electrode polarization with increasing current density further confirm the robust stability and excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage ability of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3f</a>). Moreover, the rate abilities and cycling performance of other Se<sub>1-x</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>x</sub>@SC composites are compared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The reversible capacities of 472.2 and 460.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained after 200 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for Se<sub>0.25</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.75</sub>@SC and Se<sub>0.5</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.5</sub>@SC, respectively. The results highlight that the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode, with a higher level of Se substitution, achieves the highest reversible capacity among Se<sub>0.25</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.75</sub>@SC and Se<sub>0.5</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.5</sub>@SC electrodes. This suggests that increasing the amount of Se atoms in Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC increases the Na<sup>+</sup> active sites and stabilizes the formation of Na<sub>y</sub>Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC.</p>
         
         <p>For practical applications as an anode material, achieving long-term cycling stability is crucial. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3g</a>, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC presents a high reversible capacity of 515&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>after 1250 cycles at high current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, maintaining a CE of 100% throughout the cycling. In contrast, the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC and FeSe@C exhibit poor cycling stability after 1250 cycles. Notably, Se<sub>0.5</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.5</sub>@SC exhibits significantly improved specific capacity during cycling, attributed primarily to enhanced electrode-electrolyte interfacial contact.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0027_R_d99263522e1553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energies Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it can be concluded that the improved intercalation-conversion reaction kinetics induced by Se incorporation into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC enhances electrode activation during cycles, thereby reducing undesirable reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0028_R_d99263522e1560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Nano">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparative analysis with previous studies on iron-based chalcogenides and other transition metal dichalcogenide anode materials for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3h</a>) demonstrates that Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC delivers higher capacity and superior long-term cycling performance, highlighting its potential as an advanced material for sodium storage applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0003_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano Mater. Today Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Phys. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0009_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Electroanal. Chem. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0013_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0022_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0025_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chin. Chem. Lett. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Eng. J. Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0026_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0029_R_d99263522e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Motivated by the outstanding Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC in half-cells, sodium ion full-cells were fabricated by employing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (NVP/C) as cathodes and Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC as anodes. Electrochemical properties of NVP/C half-cells are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a–c</a> (Supporting Information). As shown in GCD profiles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3i</a>), the NVP/C cathode and Se-Fe1-xS@C anode display a high charge voltage platform of 3.14&nbsp;V and low discharge voltage platform of 0.4&nbsp;V respectively. To balance capacity, the active mass ratio of cathode/anode was adjusted to 5:1, and the voltage range set at 0.4-3.8&nbsp;V in the NVP/C//Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC full cell. The results show that the full cell exhibited an initial charge capacity of 527.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and a charge/discharge capacity of 479.7/509.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with excellent ICE of 94.0% in the second cycle (calculated based on anode mass). The NVP/C//Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC full cell displayed high-rate performance with capacities of 495.6, 406.8, and 337.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at current density 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> respectively, and delivered a reversible capacity of 284&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the full-cell maintained an outstanding reversible capacity of 302.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3k</a>). In addition, a series of light-emitting diode (LED) could be easily powered by the coin-type NVP/C//Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC full cell (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0003">3k</a>), demonstrating the potential of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC as an anode material for future applications.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite, in situ/ ex situ tests were conducted. As shown in in situ XRD patterns (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0004">4a</a>), several strong peaks located at ≈38.0°, 41.5°, and 43.5° belong to the Be and BeO. The contour plot reveals prominent diffraction peaks at 28.7°, 33.7°, and 43.5° in the pristine Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>, corresponding to the (200), (2011), and (2022) planes, confirming the stable electrode structure of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@C. As the discharge proceeds to 1.2&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe1-xS<sub>0.25</sub> gradually shift to a lower angle, indicative of crystal structure expansion due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>, potentially forming intermediate Na<sub>y</sub>Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>. As the electrode continues to discharge deeply (1.0&nbsp;V), a new peak at 44.2° emerges, corresponding to the (311) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, along with peaks of intermediate products. Meanwhile, the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se is confirmed at the middle of discharging, with a weak peak at ≈39° belonging to the (220) plane of (220) of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0020_R_d99263522e1731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 45.8° can be associated with metal Fe (JCPD 88–2324). When the electrode is completely discharged to the final potential of 0.4&nbsp;V, the signals of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se remain. The in situ XRD results indicate that Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> undergoes intercalation and conversion reactions, resulting in highly stabilized products of Fe, Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. During the charge process (2.0&nbsp;V), the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se disappear, indicating high reversibility. As the electrode charges to the final potential of 3.0&nbsp;V, the original crystalline phase of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> is completely recovered, consistent with other reported iron-based compound electrodes for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0010_R_d99263522e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Nano-Micro Lett. ACS Sustain Chem Eng">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0014_R_d99263522e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Chem. Eng. J.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0016_R_d99263522e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Omega">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0029_R_d99263522e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0030_R_d99263522e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Small Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414246-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b5c6271-4649-41e7-88c5-78ef59ddc440/adfm202414246-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b5c6271-4649-41e7-88c5-78ef59ddc440/adfm202414246-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6a94fecc-414f-4603-affc-6c83922edf21/adfm202414246-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b5c6271-4649-41e7-88c5-78ef59ddc440/adfm202414246-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414246-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Reaction mechanism of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC. a) Initial GCD and in situ XRD contour map during the initial cycle. Ex situ HRTEM images at different initial discharge states: b) discharge state of 1.0&nbsp;V, c) Discharge state of 0.4&nbsp;V, and d) Charge state of 3.0&nbsp;V. e) The related schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The phase transformation of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC can also be examined by ex situ TEM. When discharged to 1.0&nbsp;V, the electrode shows detectable lattice fringes with interplanar spacings of 0.21 and 0.269&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (311) crystal phase of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and the (211) crystal phase of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0004">4b</a>). At a fully discharge state of 0.4&nbsp;V, the ultimate products, with the crystal spacings of 0.20, 220, and 0.21&nbsp;nm, can be associated with the (110) plane of metallic Fe, the (220) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the (311) of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0004">4c</a>), confirming the occurrence of conversion reaction during the sodium storage process of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>. These results indicating that the in situ formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se not only stabilizes the reaction products of Fe<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S during the intercalation and conversion process but also provides more diffusion paths for sodium ion migration and electron transport while reducing the dissolution of sulfur. Moreover, the TEM and EDX elemental mapping results shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) reveal that Fe, S, and Se elements are uniformly distributed over the charged sample, proving excellent structural stability during the intercalation and conversion reaction processes. Additionally, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> hollow nanospheres embedded within the carbon shell are well-preserved even after 300 cycles at a current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, further indicating that the carbon layer effectively prevents volume expansion and structural collapse of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC during the repeated ionic insertion/de-insertion. When the electrode is fully charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, a detectable crystal phase of 0.269&nbsp;nm, assigned to the (2011) plane of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>, indicates high reversible phase transformation during the conversion reaction. Based on the above investigations, the related intercalation-conversion mechanism in Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC can be schematically illustrated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0004">4e</a>) and defined by the following reaction:</p>

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}3{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{Se + 3N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{S\ + \ }}\left( {{\mathrm{4-4x}}} \right){\mathrm{Fe\ }} \to {\mathrm{\ 4N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{y}}}{\mathrm{S}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.75}}}}{\mathrm{-F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{1-x}}}}{{{\mathrm{S}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.25\ }}}} \nonumber\\+ \left( {{\mathrm{8-4y}}} \right){\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + } + {\mathrm{(8-4y)}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}^{\mathrm{-}}}\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202414246-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/644b90af-bd76-4518-9c55-19bcc1a76945/adfm202414246-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202414246:adfm202414246-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202414246-math-0004.png">

                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>

                           </msub>
                           <msub>

                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <mrow>

                              <mi>yN</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mrow>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">y</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}_{\mathrm{y}}}{\mathrm{S}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.75}}}}{\mathrm{-F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{1-x}}}}{{{\mathrm{S}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.25}}}}\ \to {\mathrm{\ S}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.75}}}}{\mathrm{-F}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}_{{\mathrm{1-x}}}}{{{\mathrm{S}}}_{{\mathrm{0}}{\mathrm{.25}}}}{\mathrm{\ + \ yN}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }{\mathrm{\ + \ y}}{{{\mathrm{e}}}^{\mathrm{-}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to gain insight into the fundamental effect of Se incorporation in Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S on Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability at the atomic level using the first-principles calculations. As presents in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, the optimized surfaces of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S reveals that Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> exhibits a much lower adsorption energy of −2.75&nbsp;eV and a conversion energy of −1.67&nbsp;eV, compared with −1.81 and −1.53&nbsp;eV of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, respectively. This suggests that Se substitution results in a more energetically favorable adsorption of Na atoms and conversion to Na<sub>2</sub>Se, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Fe. The charge density difference analysis was conducted to understand the atomic bonding properties of adsorbed Na<sup>+</sup> on the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5d,e</a>, a wider charge transfer region of Na<sup>+</sup> is observed around the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> compared to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, indicating a stronger interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>. This demonstrates the effectiveness of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on these active sites and synergistically boosts Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability. Additionally, Bader charge analysis further suggests that S in Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> donates more electrons (0.04 e<sup>−</sup>) than S in Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (0.02 e<sup>−</sup>) after Na adsorption, in align with the enhanced binding between Na and Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The results of adsorption-conversion energies, and charge density differences revealed that the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC with dual vacancy presents much lower adsorption-conversion energies and wider charge density differences compared to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC with only cationic Fe vacancy. Therefore, the incorporation of Se anion defects into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S can create new active sites and modulate the electronic structure of Fe<sup>0</sup>, facilitating fast intercalation-conversion reaction kinetics for Na<sup>+</sup> storage and guaranteeing long cycle life with high capacity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202414246-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e441186b-9085-40aa-ad91-056971be4a9a/adfm202414246-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e441186b-9085-40aa-ad91-056971be4a9a/adfm202414246-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ea6a072-ef81-45e8-b1aa-cc0637e8a039/adfm202414246-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e441186b-9085-40aa-ad91-056971be4a9a/adfm202414246-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202414246-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202414246" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Optimized surfaces of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC for Na adsorption. c) Adsorption and conversion energies for Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC. d,e) Charge densities, and f) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10th cycles for Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC. g) CV curves at different scan rates and h) Pseudocapacitive contribution at 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC. i) Calculated pseudocapacitance and diffusion contributions of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration techniques (GITT) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted to gain a deeper understanding of the reaction kinetics. As observed, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode displays a larger diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) and lower overpotentials than Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC through the redox process, indicating significantly improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information). During the initial discharge phase, the calculated diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) for Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC reaches a high value of 8.12&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−8</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup> and gradually decreasing thereafter, which signifies rapid insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC. Noteworthy, at the voltage window (0.75&nbsp;V), the curves show a gradual rise before continuing to drop to the lowest D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value of 9.32&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−8</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;S<sup>−1</sup>, indicating beneficial effects of D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> on the conversion reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0031_R_d99263522e2580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As charging progresses, D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> gradually rises again, reaching its peak value upon completion of the charging process. Compared to Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC displays much more stable D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values during the charge/discharge process. The Nyquist plots of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and Fe1-xS@SC after cycling at show similar outlines, where the semicircle represents charge transfer resistance R<sub>ct</sub>, R<sub>s</sub> and CPE are associated to the electrolyte resistance and double-layer capacitance, respectively, while the low-frequency sloping line corresponds to the Warburg impedance (w) of Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0032_R_d99263522e2614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the fitting results of R<sub>ct</sub>, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode exhibits a much lower charge transfer resistance value (17&nbsp;Ω) compared to the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC electrode (154&nbsp;Ω), indicating that Se incorporation enhances interfacial charge transfer kinetics by improving electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202414246-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202414246-bib-0033_R_d99263522e2630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results confirm that Se incorporation into Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC provides more active sites, facilitates fast electron transfer, enhances electrical conductivity, improves conversion reaction kinetics, and lowers the barrier to sodium ion diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>To further comprehend the origin of the excellent Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage reaction kinetic of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, the CV profiles of the electrode were collected at different sweep rates. The Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode displays similar CV curve shape with slightly prominent peaks shifts across stepwise increasing sweep rates from 0.2 to 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5g</a>), indicating smaller polarization during the charge/discharge process. The calculated <i>b</i>-values for peak 1, peak 2, and peak 3 of the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC anode are 0.93, 0.90, and 1.00, respectively, which are higher than those of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC (0.83 for peak 1, 0.89 for peak 2, and 0.90 for peak 3), suggesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism is predominantly controlled by the surface capacitive reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a–c</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode exhibits a high capacitive contribution of 86.3% at 0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5h</a>). The percentage capacitive contribution increases from 74.6% to 92.5% with the scan rate from 0.2 to 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than the Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S@SC, which ranges from 63.2% to 81.5% at a similar scan rate. This demonstrates that the incorporation of Se species into metal sulfide could improve pseudocapacitive charge storage, which is beneficial for fast reaction kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202414246-fig-0005">5i</a>). These advantages endow the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC with a more favorable surface-induced pseudo-capacitance charge storage, exhibiting improved rate capability for fast-charge SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202414246-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202414246-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully designed and fabricated Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC hollow nanospheres structures composite through an in situ synchronous sulfuration/selenization/carbonization strategy. The systematic experimental studies and DFT calculations were employed to investigate the role of Se vacancies in the kinetics performance of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC composite. The introduction of Se species not only distorts the interlayer spacing and enhances the intrinsic conductivity of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC, but also improves Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy and provides more active sites, greatly promoting the intercalation/conversion pseudocapacitance contribution and accelerating redox reaction kinetics. The polar C─S, Fe─S─C and C─Se chemical bonds together with the hollow architecture, further guarantees fast electron transportation channels and strong structural integrity, mitigating the volume variation of Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC and supporting highly reversible phase transitions during the sodiation/desodiation process. These merits of combined structural and vacancy engineering endow the Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC electrode with excellent cycling stability (515&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1250 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and outstanding rate performance (418&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). More importantly, NVP/C//Se<sub>0.75</sub>-Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S<sub>0.25</sub>@SC full-cells demonstrate the feasibility of practical application. This work provides a promising design of dual vacancy engineering to realize advanced anode materials with excellent electrochemical properties for next-generation energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
